Bathing and Seeking Shade for the Muhrim
Rulings on bathing and shade for muhrim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the permissibility of bathing and seeking shade for a muhrim and explain the legal reasoning based on analogy.

Core Idea
Ihram prohibits certain garments but not purification or shade that does not touch the body.

This lesson covers two basic acts that are permissible for a person in ihram: bathing and seeking shade. The text explains that bathing is allowed because it is purification, and shade is allowed via a structure that does not touch the body, analogous to a house.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
Read the first part of the source text. Identify the two acts: bathing (including entering a hammam) and seeking shade under a house or a mahmil. Note that both are ruled permissible (la ba's).
2
Locate the underlying illa (reason)
For bathing, the text states it is an act of purification (taharah), which is not prohibited during ihram. For shade, the reason is that the mahmil does not touch the body (la yamassu badanahu), so it is like a house.
The illa for shade is the lack of direct contact with the body.
3
Understand the qiyas (analogy)
Bathing is permitted because purification is inherently allowed; the muhrim is not prevented from acts of purification. Shade via a mahmil is analogous to shade of a house because both provide cover without touching the body. This is an example of qiyas jali (clear analogy).
The principle is that any structure not contacting the body is permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( ولا بأس أن يغتسل، ويدخل الحمام)، ولأن الغسل طهارة، فلا يُمنع منها. * قوله: (ويستظل بالبيت والمَحْمِل؛ لأن المَحْمِلَ لا يَمس بدنه، فأشبه البيت. قوله: (ويَشُدَّ في وسطه (الهمْيان بالكسر، وهو شيء تُجعل فيه الدراهم، ويُشدُّ على الحقو. - وكذا له أن يشد المنطقة. وعن أبي يوسف : كراهتها إذا شدَّها بإبزيم؛ لأنه يشبه المخيط، كمن لبس الطيلسان وزره عليه. * قوله: (ولا يغسل رأسه، ولا لحيته بالخطمي). ويجوز للمحرمة أن تلبس الحرير، والحلي. كذا في «الكرخي». قاسم في التعريف والإخبار ٤٤٥/٣ : أخرجه ابن منده في الصحابة.
His statement: (There is no harm in his bathing and entering the hammam), and because bathing is purification, so he should not be prevented from it. * His statement: (And he may seek shade under a house or a mahmil) because the mahmil does not touch his body, so it is like a house. His statement: (And he may tie a himyan around his waist) – himyan is a thing in which dirhams are placed and tied around the waist. - And likewise he may tie a mintaqah. And from Abu Yusuf: it is disliked if he ties it with a buckle, because it resembles a stitched garment, like one who wears a taylasan and buttons it. * His statement: (And he should not wash his head or his beard with khitmi). And it is permissible for a muhrimah (female in ihram) to wear silk and jewelry. Thus in al-Karkhi. Qasim in al-Ta'rif wa al-Ikhabar 3/445: Ibn Mandah extracted it in al-Sahabah.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of several rulings for a person in ihram (muhrim). First, it is permissible to bathe and enter a hammam (public bath) because bathing is an act of purification, and purification is not prohibited during ihram. Second, the muhrim may seek shade under a house or a mahmil (a litter on a camel) because the mahmil does not directly touch the body, similar to a house; i.e., it acts as a barrier. Third, the muhrim may tie a himyan (a money belt or pouch for dirhams) around his waist. Similarly, he may tie a mintaqah (a belt or waistband). However, according to Abu Yusuf, if the belt has a buckle (ibzim), it is disliked because it resembles a stitched garment (makhit), similar to wearing a taylasan (a hooded cloak) and buttoning it. Fourth, the muhrim must not wash his head or beard with khitmi (marshmallow root, which produces a lather) as it is a cleansing agent. Finally, a female muhrimah is allowed to wear silk and jewelry, as mentioned in al-Karkhi.

Key Terms
المحمل (al-mahmil): A covered litter on a camel that provides shade; permissible to use because it does not touch the body directly.
الغسل (ghusl): Bathing or washing the entire body; in ihram, it is permissible as an act of purification.
طهارة (taharah): Purification; the reason bathing is allowed in ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it permissible for a muhrim to seek shade under a mahmil?
A) Because it is a form of worship.
B) Because it does not touch the body, similar to a house.
C) Because it is made of cloth, which is allowed.
D) Because it is a traditional practice of the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Bathing and non-contact shade are permissible for a muhrim; the key is avoiding anything that touches the body like sewn garments.

Permissible and Impermissible Garments and Cleansing for the Muhrim
Rulings on belts, cleansing, and women's attire in ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify permissible belts and prohibited cleansing agents for a muhrim, and apply Abu Yusuf's ruling on buckled belts.

Core Idea
A muhrim may wear a money belt but a belt with a buckle is disliked by Abu Yusuf due to resemblance to stitched clothes.

This lesson addresses three distinct items: wearing a himyan (money belt) or mintaqah (belt) — with an intra-Hanafi nuance on buckles, the prohibition of washing the head or beard with khitmi (marshmallow root), and the special permissibility for women to wear silk and jewelry in ihram. These rulings highlight the principle that items resembling stitched garments or used for cleaning are restricted.

Guided Path
1
Identify each item and its ruling
Read the second part of the passage. Identify: (1) himyan/money belt – permissible; (2) mintaqah/belt – permissible, but Abu Yusuf dislikes it if buckled; (3) khitmi – prohibited on head/beard; (4) silk and jewelry for women – permissible.
Note that the belt ruling includes an intra-madhhab difference.
2
Understand the illa for each ruling
For belts: permissible because they are not sewn garments; the buckle resemblance to makhit causes dislike according to Abu Yusuf. For khitmi: it is a cleansing agent that lathers, akin to soap, thus prohibited. For women: silk and jewelry are exempted as they are adornments normal for women.
The illa for prohibition of khitmi is that it cleanses in a way not allowed; for belt buckle, it is resemblance to sewn garment.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference on belt buckle
The matn states the general permissibility of tying a mintaqah. Abu Yusuf adds a qualification: if it has a buckle (ibzim), it is makruh because it resembles a stitched garment. The other Hanafi masters (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) likely maintain permissibility without dislike. The practical difference: which view is followed in fatwa?
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is often the permissibility, as the buckle is not the same as sewing.
4
Apply the gender-specific rule
Note that women in ihram are allowed to wear silk and jewelry, while men cannot wear silk. This shows that prohibitions in ihram are not uniform by gender.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( ولا بأس أن يغتسل، ويدخل الحمام)، ولأن الغسل طهارة، فلا يُمنع منها. * قوله: (ويستظل بالبيت والمَحْمِل؛ لأن المَحْمِلَ لا يَمس بدنه، فأشبه البيت. قوله: (ويَشُدَّ في وسطه (الهمْيان بالكسر، وهو شيء تُجعل فيه الدراهم، ويُشدُّ على الحقو. - وكذا له أن يشد المنطقة. وعن أبي يوسف : كراهتها إذا شدَّها بإبزيم؛ لأنه يشبه المخيط، كمن لبس الطيلسان وزره عليه. * قوله: (ولا يغسل رأسه، ولا لحيته بالخطمي). ويجوز للمحرمة أن تلبس الحرير، والحلي. كذا في «الكرخي». قاسم في التعريف والإخبار ٤٤٥/٣ : أخرجه ابن منده في الصحابة.
His statement: (There is no harm in his bathing and entering the hammam), and because bathing is purification, so he should not be prevented from it. * His statement: (And he may seek shade under a house or a mahmil) because the mahmil does not touch his body, so it is like a house. His statement: (And he may tie a himyan around his waist) – himyan is a thing in which dirhams are placed and tied around the waist. - And likewise he may tie a mintaqah. And from Abu Yusuf: it is disliked if he ties it with a buckle, because it resembles a stitched garment, like one who wears a taylasan and buttons it. * His statement: (And he should not wash his head or his beard with khitmi). And it is permissible for a muhrimah (female in ihram) to wear silk and jewelry. Thus in al-Karkhi. Qasim in al-Ta'rif wa al-Ikhabar 3/445: Ibn Mandah extracted it in al-Sahabah.

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of several rulings for a person in ihram (muhrim). First, it is permissible to bathe and enter a hammam (public bath) because bathing is an act of purification, and purification is not prohibited during ihram. Second, the muhrim may seek shade under a house or a mahmil (a litter on a camel) because the mahmil does not directly touch the body, similar to a house; i.e., it acts as a barrier. Third, the muhrim may tie a himyan (a money belt or pouch for dirhams) around his waist. Similarly, he may tie a mintaqah (a belt or waistband). However, according to Abu Yusuf, if the belt has a buckle (ibzim), it is disliked because it resembles a stitched garment (makhit), similar to wearing a taylasan (a hooded cloak) and buttoning it. Finally, a female muhrimah is allowed to wear silk and jewelry, as mentioned in al-Karkhi.

Key Terms
الهميان (al-himyan): A money belt or pouch tied around the waist; permissible for muhrim.
المنطقة (al-mintaqah): A belt or waistband; permissible, but disliked with a buckle according to Abu Yusuf.
الإبزيم (al-ibzim): A buckle or clasp; disliked on a belt for muhrim because it resembles stitched garment.
الخطمي (al-khitmi): Marshmallow root, used as a cleansing agent; prohibited for muhrim to use on head or beard.
المحرمة (al-muhrimah): Female in a state of ihram; she is permitted to wear silk and jewelry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, what is the ruling on a male muhrim washing his beard with khitmi?
A) Permissible because it is like water.
B) Makruh because it cleans too much.

D) Permissible only for women.

Key Takeaway
The muhrim may use a money belt, but should avoid buckled belts per one scholar; must not wash head/beard with khitmi; women may wear silk and jewelry.

Permissibility of Bathing, Entering Bathhouse, and Seeking Shade in Ihram
Listing permissible acts in ihram with rational proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three permissible acts for a muhrim and explain their rational proofs as presented by al-Quduri and the commentator.

Core Idea
Acts of purification and non-contact shade are permissible in ihram because they do not violate prohibitions.

In ihram, many ordinary actions become restricted to maintain the state of sanctity. However, not every action is prohibited. Al-Quduri lists three permissible acts: bathing, entering a bathhouse, and seeking shade under a fixed structure or howdah. The commentator provides the rationale: bathing is an act of purification, and the howdah does not touch the body, analogous to a house. Understanding these rulings helps the muhrim distinguish between legitimate care and prohibited indulgence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Permissible Acts
Read the passage and list the three actions: bathing (ghusl), entering a bathhouse (hammam), and seeking shade under a house or howdah.
These are all listed under the phrase 'لا بأس' – indicates permissibility.
2
Analyze the Proof for Bathing
The commentary states: 'لأن الغسل طهارة' – because bathing is purification. Understand that purification (taharah) is a recommended or obligatory act that cannot be prohibited in ihram.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli): the purpose of Hajj includes ritual purity, so preventing purification would contradict the spirit of worship.
3
Analyze the Proof for Seeking Shade
The howdah is analogized to a house: 'لأن المحمل لا يمس بدنه، فأشبه البيت.' The key difference is direct contact. Anything that touches the head or body is prohibited, but fixed structures like houses and howdahs that do not touch are allowed.
This is a qiyas (analogical reasoning) based on the illah of non-contact.
4
Determine the Ruling Level
These acts are mubah (permissible) without penalty. There is no sin or compensation required when done correctly.
However, if a prohibited act is committed (e.g., using perfume in the bathhouse), the permissibility is void.
5
Apply to Modern Scenarios
Recognize that today, a muhrim may take an unscented shower, use a fixed umbrella if it does not touch the head, or sit under a roof. But a moving parasol that contacts the head is prohibited.
The principle remains: avoid direct covering and scented products.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بأس أن يغتسل، ويدخل الحمام ، ويستظل بالبيت، والمَحْمِل. قوله: ( ولا بأس أن يغتسل، ويدخل الحمام)، ولأن الغسل طهارة، فلا يُمنع منها. * قوله: (ويستظل بالبيت والمَحْمِل؛ لأن المَحْمِلَ لا يَمس بدنه، فأشبه البيت.
And there is no harm in bathing, entering the bathhouse, and seeking shade under a house and the howdah. He said: (And there is no harm in bathing and entering the bathhouse), because bathing is purification, so he is not prevented from it. * He said: (And seeking shade under a house and the howdah), because the howdah does not touch his body, so it resembles the house.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage addresses three permissible acts for a muhrim: bathing, entering a bathhouse (hammam), and seeking shade under a fixed structure or a howdah. The commentator provides the rationale: bathing is an act of purification (taharah) and therefore cannot be prohibited. Seeking shade from a house or howdah is allowed because the howdah, like a house, does not make direct contact with the body, thus avoids the prohibition of covering the head or body with something that touches.

Key Terms
يغتسل (yaghtasil): To perform a full body wash (ghusl).
الحمام (al-hammam): A bathhouse or place for bathing.
يستظل (yastazill): To seek shade (under a roof or canopy).
المحمل (al-mahmil): A howdah (camel litter) used for transport; it has a canopy that does not directly touch the rider's body.
طهارة (taharah): Purification, both physical and ritual.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it permissible for a muhrim to enter a bathhouse according to the commentary?
A) Because it is a necessity
B) Because bathing is purification, and purification is not prevented in ihram
C) Because the bathhouse is private
D) Because it is not explicitly prohibited

Key Takeaway
Bathing and seeking shade under non-touching covers are permissible in ihram because they do not conflict with the prohibitions of covering or scenting.

The Money Belt in Ihram: Permissibility and the Dispute Over Buckles
Stating permissibility of money belt with a caution about buckles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tying a money belt in ihram and distinguish Abu Yusuf's position on buckles, applying the analogy to sewn garments.

Core Idea
The himyan is allowed in ihram, but Abu Yusuf cautions that a buckle resembles sewn garments and is disliked.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah addresses the permissibility for a muhrim to tie a money belt (himyan) or a belt (mintaqah) around his waist. While the text affirms its permissibility, it also records an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf considers it disliked (makruh) if the belt has a buckle, analogizing it to sewn garments (makhit). This lesson unpacks the ruling and the reasoning behind the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The matn states that a muhrim may tighten a himyan (money belt) around his waist. The default ruling is permissibility (mubah).
Himyan is a pouch for coins tied around the waist.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that if the belt is tied with a buckle (ibzim), it becomes disliked (makruh). This is the disputed point.
Other Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) do not restrict it.
3
Analyze the analogy (qiyas)
Abu Yusuf compares a belt with a buckle to a buttoned taylasan (cloak), which resembles a sewn garment. Both involve fasteners that create a tailored fit.
The illa (legal cause) is resemblance to makhit (sewn garments).
4
Understand the concept of makhit
Sewn garments are prohibited for men in ihram because they are tailored to the body. A buckle creates a similar fitted shape, hence the dislike.
The taylasan analogy reinforces the reasoning.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentary does not explicitly prefer one view, but the permissibility without restriction is the more common and relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
Abu Yusuf's view is a cautionary opinion within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويَشدَّ في وسطه الهِمْيان . قوله: (ويَشدَّ في وسطه (الهمْيان بالكسر، وهو شيء تُجعل فيه الدراهم، ويُشدُّ على الحقو. - وكذا له أن يشد المنطقة. وعن أبي يوسف : كراهتها إذا شدَّها بإبزيم؛ لأنه يشبه المخيط، كمن لبس الطيلسان وزره عليه.
And he may tie the himyan around his waist. He said: (And he ties around his waist) al-himyan (with kasra on the ha) — it is something in which dirhams are placed and it is tied on the waist. — And similarly he may tie the mintaqah (belt). And from Abu Yusuf: it is disliked if he ties it with a buckle because it resembles a sewn garment, like one who wears a taylasan and buttons it.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
The matn states that a muhrim may tighten a himyan (money belt) around his waist. The commentator explains the term himyan as a pouch for coins, and notes that a belt (mintaqah) is also permissible. However, Abu Yusuf holds that it is makruh (disliked) if the belt has a buckle, as it resembles sewn garments (makhit) which are prohibited for men. The analogy is to a taylasan (a type of cloak) that is buttoned, which would also be disliked. This shows that the prohibition of sewn garments extends to any item that is stitched or closed with fasteners.

Key Terms
الهميان (al-himyan): A money belt or pouch tied around the waist to hold coins.
المنطقة (al-mintaqah): A belt or girdle worn around the waist.
الإبزيم (al-ibzim): A buckle or clasp used to fasten a belt.
المخيط (al-makhit): Sewn garments; items that are stitched and tailored to the body.
الطيلسان (al-taylasan): A type of cloak or mantle, often buttoned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, when is tying a belt disliked in ihram?
A) When it is tightly tied
B) When it has a buckle
C) When it is made of leather
D) When it is worn over the ihram garments

Key Takeaway
The himyan is allowed in ihram; buckles are disliked according to Abu Yusuf but the majority view permits them.

Permissibility of Silk and Jewelry for Women in Ihram
Stating a special permission for women in ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wearing silk and jewelry for a woman in ihram and distinguish it from the ruling for men.

Core Idea
Women in ihram may wear silk and jewelry; men in ihram may not.

A common question about ihram is whether women can wear silk and jewelry. Many assume that ihram prohibits all adornments, but the Hanafi school makes an exception for women. This lesson explains that permission and clarifies the difference between the rulings for men and women.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actor
The passage specifies 'al-muhrimah' — a woman who has entered ihram. Identify who this ruling applies to.
2
Identify the acts permitted
Two items are mentioned: silk fabric (al-harir) and jewelry (al-huli). Note that these are typically prohibited for men in ihram.
3
Note the ruling
The verb 'tajuzu' indicates permissibility (ibaha). So the ruling is mubah: allowed, not mandatory or recommended.
4
Note the authority
The passage ends with 'kadha fi al-Karkhi', citing the Hanafi jurist al-Karkhi as the source. This indicates the ruling is well-established in the school.
5
Distinguish from men's ruling
Contrast this with the prohibition for men: men cannot wear sewn garments, silk, or gold. Women have a dispensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز للمحرمة أن تلبس الحرير، والحلي. كذا في «الكرخي».
And it is permissible for a woman in ihram to wear silk and jewelry. Thus in al-Karkhi.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This short ruling grants a dispensation for women that is not available to men. The prohibition of sewn garments and ornaments for men is strict; women may wear silk and jewelry because these are part of their general adornment and covering. The attribution to al-Karkhi indicates this is a well-established view in the Hanafi school. There is no further commentary here.

Key Terms
المحرمة (al-muhrimah): A woman who has entered the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah.
الحرير (al-harir): Silk fabric; women are allowed to wear it in ihram.
الحلي (al-huli): Jewelry (e.g., gold, silver ornaments).
الكرخي (al-Karkhi): Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi (d. 340 AH), a prominent Hanafi jurist whose works are authoritative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who is allowed to wear silk and jewelry in ihram according to this passage?
A) Men and women
B) Only men
C) Only women
D) Neither men nor women

Key Takeaway
A woman in ihram may wear silk and jewelry; a man may not.

Prohibition of Washing with Khitmi in Ihram and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Penalty
Stating prohibition and penalty dispute, plus consensus on sidr/soap

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using khitmi in ihram and distinguish the penalty positions of Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Using a fragrant soap (khitmi) in ihram is prohibited; the penalty is either a dam (Abu Hanifah) or sadaqah (Sahibayn).

You already know that a muhrim must avoid perfume and acts that remove dirt or kill lice. This lesson addresses the use of khitmi, a fragrant plant used for washing. While all Hanafi imams agree it is prohibited, they differ on the required penalty. We will explore the reasoning behind each view and the practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage prohibits washing the head or beard with khitmi. The ruling is haram (prohibited) because it has a pleasing fragrance, removes dirt, and kills lice.
2
Understand the reasoning (talil) for prohibition
Khitmi is compared to henna (also prohibited) due to its scent. Additionally, it removes tafath (dirt) and kills hawam (lice), both of which are restricted in ihram.
3
Compare the penalty views
Abu Hanifah holds that a dam (blood sacrifice) is required because the fragrance makes it similar to using perfume. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that sadaqah (charity) suffices because the primary purpose is cleaning.
The difference hinges on whether fragrance or cleaning is given more weight.
4
Note the consensus on sidr and soap
The scholars agree that using sidr (lotus leaves) or soap incurs no penalty because they lack fragrance. This shows that the prohibition is specifically tied to scent.
This consensus (ijma) provides a clear boundary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يَغْسل رأسه، ولا لحيته بالخِطْمِي. قوله: (ولا يغسل رأسه، ولا لحيته بالخطمي). فإن فَعَلَ فعليه دم عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن الخطمي له رائحة مستلذة، فهو كالحنَّاء، ولأنه يُزيل التَّفَث، ويقتل الهوام. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد عليه صدقة؛ لأنه يُزيل الوسخ، ويقتل الهوام. وأجمعوا على أنه إذا غسله بالسدر ، أو بالصابون: لا شيء عليه. والرجال والنساء في اجتناب الطيب: سواء. وإنما يختلفان في لبس المخيط، وتغطية الرأس: فإن المرأة تفعلهما، دون الرجل؛ لأنها عورة. قاسم في التعريف والإخبار ٤٤٥/٣ : أخرجه ابن منده في الصحابة.
And he should not wash his head or his beard with khitmi. He said: (And he does not wash his head or his beard with khitmi). If he does so, then he must offer a dam (sacrifice) according to Abu Hanifah, because khitmi has a pleasant fragrance, so it is like henna, and because it removes dirt and kills lice. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: he must give sadaqah (charity), because it removes filth and kills lice. And they are unanimously agreed that if he washes with sidr or soap, nothing is upon him. Men and women are equal in avoiding perfume. They only differ in wearing sewn garments and covering the head: the woman may do both, unlike the man, because she is 'awrah. Qasim in al-Ta'rif wa al-Ikhabar 3/445: It was narrated by Ibn Mandah in al-Sahabah.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage prohibits the use of khitmi (a fragrant plant used as soap) for washing the head and beard in ihram. If a muhrim violates this, the penalty differs among the Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifah requires a dam (blood sacrifice), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) consider only sadaqah (charity) obligatory. The rationale for Abu Hanifah is that khitmi has a pleasant scent like henna, and it removes dirt and kills lice — acts that are otherwise prohibited or at least discouraged. The two companions argue that since it primarily removes filth, the penalty is lesser. There is consensus (ijma) that using sidr (lotus leaves) or soap incurs no penalty, as these have no fragrance. The passage then notes equality of men and women in avoiding perfume, but differences in sewn garments and head covering: women may wear them because their entire body is 'awrah, so covering is not a violation of ihram.

Key Terms
الخطمي (al-khitmi): A plant (Althaea officinalis) used as a soap; has a pleasant fragrance.
دم (dam): A blood sacrifice (a sheep or goat) required as penalty for certain violations in Hajj.
صدقة (sadaqah): Charity, specifically one mudd of wheat or equivalent per violation.
السدر (al-sidr): Leaves of the lotus tree (Ziziphus spina-christi); used as a soap without fragrance.
التفث (al-tafath): Dirt, filth, and general uncleanliness of the body.
الهوام (al-hawam): Pests, especially lice; killing them is prohibited for the muhrim.

Key Takeaway
Using khitmi in ihram is haram; Abu Hanifah requires a dam, while the Sahibayn require sadaqah; sidr and soap incur no penalty.

Increasing Talbiyah After Prayers and Raising the Voice
Encouraging talbiyah after prayers and raising voice, with intra-Hanafi scope difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on increasing talbiyah after prayers, identify the hadith proof, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference on which prayers it applies to.

Core Idea
Talbiyah after every prayer is recommended; raising the voice is part of the best Hajj.

Talbiyah is a distinctive act of Hajj that continues throughout the state of ihram. The Mukhtasar al-Quduri encourages increasing it after prayers and raising the voice. The commentary of al-Jawhara al-Nayyira expands on this with a hadith and notes a difference among later Hanafi scholars on whether this applies after all prayers or only the obligatory ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended acts
Locate the two acts: increasing talbiyah after prayers, and raising the voice with it. Note that both are categorized as mustahab (recommended) in the Hanafi school.
The text explicitly says 'wa yukthiru min al-talbiyah...' and 'wa al-mustahab an yarfa'a biha sawtahu'.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith cited: 'Afdal al-Hajj al-'ajj wa al-thajj'. Identify the source reference from the footnote: Sunan al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, etc.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but considered sound by many scholars.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith indicates recommendation. The phrase 'al-'ajj' is explicitly defined as raising the voice with talbiyah. Associating it with 'best Hajj' shows its merit, not obligation.
In Hanafi usul, a commendatory phrase indicates mustahab, not wajib.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Read the two Hanafi opinions: al-Khujandi says after all prayers (fard and nafl); al-Tahawi restricts to obligatory prayers (maktubat), excluding missed prayers (fa'itat) and supererogatory prayers (nawafil).
This is an intra-Hanafi difference, not between madhahib.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Understand that both views are transmitted. The commonly practiced position is the more comprehensive one (after all prayers) to maximize reward, but the narrower view is also valid.
The text does not explicitly choose; the student should know both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُكثر من التلبية عقيب الصلوات. قوله: (ويكثر من التلبية عقيب الصلوات). والمستحب أن يرفع بها صوته، لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «أفضل الحج : العج والنج). فالعج: رفع الصوت بالتلبية، والثج: هو ثج الدماء بالذبائح، أي إسالتها. قال الخجندي: يُكثر التلبية في أدبار الصلوات، نفلاً كانت أو فرضاً. وقال الطحاوي في أدبار المكتوبات دون الفائتات والنوافل. (۱) سنن الترمذي (۸۲۷)، سنن ابن ماجه (٢٩٢٤)، المستدرك للحاكم ٤٥١/١، وله عدة طرق فيها كلام لكن يتقوى ،بمجموعها، ينظر البدر المنير ٣١٦/١٥، وعزاه العلامة قاسم في التعريف والإخبار ١٥٦/٢ لمسند ابن أبي شيبة ٢٢٤/١ ، وقال: سنده جيد.
And he should increase the talbiyah after the prayers. He said: (And he increases the talbiyah after the prayers). And it is recommended to raise his voice with it, because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'The best Hajj is al-'ajj and al-thajj.' Al-'ajj is raising the voice with talbiyah, and al-thajj is the shedding of blood by sacrifices, i.e., causing it to flow. Al-Khujandi said: He should increase talbiyah after prayers, whether supererogatory or obligatory. Al-Tahawi said: After the obligatory prayers, excluding missed prayers and supererogatory ones. (1) Sunan al-Tirmidhi (827), Sunan Ibn Majah (2924), al-Mustadrak of al-Hakim 1/451, and it has several chains with some discussion but it is strengthened by their totality, see al-Badr al-Munir 15/316, and 'Allamah Qasim attributed it in al-Ta'rif wa al-Ikhabar 2/156 to Musnad Ibn Abi Shaybah 1/224, and said: Its chain is good.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage urges the muhrim to recite the talbiyah abundantly, especially after each prayer. It cites the hadith that the best Hajj is accompanied by raising the voice (al-'ajj) and shedding sacrificial blood (al-thajj). Raising the voice in talbiyah is recommended (mustahab). There is a difference of opinion on whether this should be after all prayers (fard and nafl) as per al-Khujandi, or only after obligatory prayers (maktubat) excluding make-up prayers (fa'itat) and supererogatory prayers (nawafil) as per al-Tahawi. The footnote provides sources for the hadith.

Key Terms
التلبية (al-talbiyah): The phrase 'Labbayk Allahumma Labbayk, Labbayka la sharika laka Labbayk...' recited repeatedly during Hajj.
العج (al-'ajj): Raising the voice with talbiyah.
الثج (al-thajj): Shedding blood of sacrificial animals.
المستحب (al-mustahab): Recommended; an act that is rewarded if done but not punished if omitted.
أدبار الصلوات (adbar al-salawat): After the completion of prayers (immediately following).
المكتوبات (al-maktubat): The obligatory five daily prayers.
الفائتات (al-fa'itat): Missed prayers that are made up (qada).
النوافل (al-nawafil): Supererogatory (voluntary) prayers.

Key Takeaway
Increasing talbiyah after prayers and raising the voice are recommended based on the hadith 'Afdal al-Hajj al-'ajj wa al-thajj', with an intra-Hanafi difference on whether it applies after all prayers or only the obligatory ones.

Using Khitmi During Ihram: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the penalty for using khitmi while in ihram with two reasoned positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the penalty for using khitmi in ihram and explain the reasoning behind each position's deduction.

Core Idea
Khitmi’s dual nature: cleanser vs. perfume — the illa determines the penalty.

In this passage, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the use of a plant called khitmi by a pilgrim in ihram. While all agree that using a scented substance requires compensation, they differ on whether the penalty is a Dam (blood sacrifice) or a lesser Sadaqah. The disagreement hinges on whether khitmi's primary attribute is its pleasant scent or its cleansing function.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is using khitmi (a type of mallow plant) while in the state of ihram. In ihram, using perfume is prohibited and using cleansing agents is generally allowed unless they have fragrance.
Khitmi is known to have both cleansing properties and a pleasant scent.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and dalil
Abu Hanifa holds that a Dam (blood sacrifice) is required. His reasoning: khitmi has a pleasant scent, so it is analogous to henna (a known perfume). Additionally, it removes filth and kills insects.
The presence of a pleasant scent is the primary illa (effective cause) for Abu Hanifa.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that only a Sadaqah (charity) is required. Their reasoning: khitmi primarily removes dirt and kills insects, not intended as perfume. Scent is secondary.
The primary function (cleansing) outweighs the incidental scent in their view.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Abu Hanifa requires a Dam (sacrifice of a sheep/goat), while the Sahibayn require Sadaqah (half a sa' of wheat or equivalent). This is a significant difference in obligation.
Dam is a heavier penalty, indicating a greater violation.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is Abu Hanifa's view (Dam) because it is more precautionary and aligns with the general principle that any scented substance in ihram triggers a dam.
This tarjih is based on caution (ihtiyat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن فَعَلَ فعليه دم عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن الخطمي له رائحة مستلذة، فهو كالحنَّاء، ولأنه يُزيل التَّفَث، ويقتل الهوام. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد عليه صدقة؛ لأنه يُزيل الوسخ، ويقتل الهوام. - وأجمعوا على أنه إذا غسله بالسدر ، أو بالصابون: لا شيء عليه.
If he does it, then upon him is Dam according to Abu Hanifa, because khitmi has a pleasant scent, so it is like henna, and because it removes filth and kills insects. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said upon him is Sadaqah, because it removes dirt and kills insects. And they all agreed that if he washes it with sidr or with soap, there is nothing upon him.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the use of khitmi (a type of plant used for cleansing) while in the state of ihram. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, using khitmi necessitates a Dam (blood sacrifice) because it has a pleasant fragrance, similar to henna, and it also serves to remove filth and kill insects. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) hold that only a Sadaqah (charity) is due, because they consider it primarily a cleansing agent, not perfume. However, all three agree that if one washes with sidr (a type of tree) or soap, there is no penalty, as these are purely cleansing and not perfumed.

Key Terms
الخَطْمِيّ (al-khitmi): A type of mallow plant used for cleansing; has a pleasant scent.
دَم (dam): A blood sacrifice (sheep or goat) required as compensation for violating ihram prohibitions.
صَدَقَة (sadaqah): Charity given to the poor, used as a lesser penalty for minor ihram violations.
أَجْمَعُوا (ajma'ū): They all agreed unanimously (the three imams).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is permissible without any penalty.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, using a scented substance in ihram triggers a penalty; whether it is Dam or Sadaqah depends on the dominant attribute of the substance.

Timing of Talbiyah After Prayers and Other Occasions
Recommendations for increasing talbiyah at various times

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi-recommended occasions for talbiyah during Hajj and identify the relied-upon view regarding after-prayer timings.

Core Idea
Talbiyah is recommended after every prayer and at key moments—ascending, descending, meeting others, and at dawn.

The passage details when a pilgrim should increase talbiyah, citing a hadith on the best Hajj. Within the Hanafi school, a slight difference exists on whether after-prayer talbiyah includes supererogatory prayers, but the relied-upon view (Zahir al-Riwaya) includes all prayers without exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
The act is increasing talbiyah, which is a sunnah muakkadah. The hadith 'Afdal al-hajj: al-‘ajj wa al-thajj' establishes the virtue of raising the voice with talbiyah.
2
List the recommended occasions
After prayers (all types), when ascending a high place, descending a valley, meeting riders, and at dawn.
3
Understand the reasoning behind occasions
Talbiyah marks transitions, akin to takbir in prayer, so it is done when moving from one state to another—including waking from sleep.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi difference
Al-Khujandi recommended talbiyah after every prayer; al-Tahawi restricted it to obligatory prayers only. The commentator sides with Zahir al-Riwaya (no distinction).
The mufta bihi position is Zahir al-Riwaya.
5
Apply the ruling
A pilgrim should recite talbiyah after each prayer (both fard and nafl), on heights and in valleys, when meeting others, and at dawn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ويكثر من التلبية عقيب الصلوات). والمستحب أن يرفع بها صوته، لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «أفضل الحج : العج والنج). فالعج: رفع الصوت بالتلبية، والثج: هو ثج الدماء بالذبائح، أي إسالتها. قال الخجندي: يُكثر التلبية في أدبار الصلوات، نفلاً كانت أو فرضاً. وقال الطحاوي في أدبار المكتوبات دون الفائتات والنوافل. جعلها بمنزلة تكبير التشريق، أما في ظاهر الرواية: في أدبار الصلوات من غير تفصيل. قوله : ( وكلَّما علا شرَفاً) : أي صَعد مكاناً مرتفعاً. قوله: (أو هَبَطَ وادياً، أو لقي ركباناً) ؛ لأن التلبية في الإحرام: على مثال التكبير في الصلوات؛ للانتقال، فيؤتى بها عند الانتقال من حال إلى حال، وكذا عند الانتباه من النوم. كذا في «الينابيع». * قوله : (وبالأسحار)، خصه ؛ لأنه وقت إجابة الدعاء.
His statement: (And he should increase talbiyah after prayers). And it is recommended to raise his voice with it, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'The best Hajj is the loud voice and the flowing of blood.' So al-'ajj is raising the voice with talbiyah, and al-thajj is the flowing of blood with sacrifices, i.e., causing it to flow. Al-Khujandi said: He increases talbiyah at the end of prayers, whether supererogatory or obligatory. And al-Tahawi said: at the end of the prescribed prayers, not the missed ones or supererogatory. He made it like the takbir of tashriq. But in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwaya), it is after prayers without specification. His statement: (and whenever he ascends a high place) meaning he climbs a high place. His statement: (or descends a valley, or meets riders) because talbiyah in ihram is like the takbir in prayers for transitions, so it is done when transitioning from one state to another, and likewise when waking from sleep. So in 'al-Yanabi''. His statement: (and at dawn), specified because it is a time of answer to supplication.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage details the recommended times for reciting the talbiyah during Hajj. The base text advises increasing talbiyah after prayers. The commentator explains that it is recommended to raise one's voice, citing a hadith that 'the best Hajj is the loud voice and the flowing of blood.' There is a difference of opinion: Al-Khujandi holds that talbiyah should be increased after every prayer, voluntary or obligatory. Al-Tahawi restricts it to after obligatory prayers only, likening it to the takbir of tashriq. However, the relied-upon view (Zahir al-Riwaya) is without such specification, applying to all prayers. Additionally, talbiyah is recommended when ascending a height, descending a valley, meeting riders, and at dawn. The reasoning is that talbiyah marks transitions, similar to takbir in prayer. Dawn is specified because it is a time when supplications are answered.

Key Terms
التلبية (al-talbiyah): The recitation of 'Labbayk Allahumma labbayk...' during Hajj and Umrah.
العج والثج (al-‘ajj wa al-thajj): Al-‘ajj: raising voice with talbiyah; al-thajj: flowing blood of sacrifices.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The outwardly transmitted and relied-upon narration from Imam Muhammad's six books.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only after obligatory prayers
B) After both obligatory and supererogatory prayers
C) Only after supererogatory prayers
D) Only after the Fajr prayer

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, talbiyah is recommended after every prayer (including supererogatory), when ascending, descending, meeting riders, and at dawn—based on the Zahir al-Riwaya position.

Entering Mecca: Beginning with the Masjid al-Haram
Ruling on entering Mecca and recommended supplication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended action upon entering Mecca and recite the associated supplication.

Core Idea
Upon entering Mecca, go directly to the Masjid al-Haram.

This lesson covers the etiquettes of entering Mecca as prescribed in the Hanafi school. The pilgrim is recommended to first head to the Sacred Mosque and recite a heartfelt supplication. The commentary also explains the names 'Makkah' and 'Bakkah', deepening our understanding of the sacred city.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
The act in question is entering Mecca, specifically for the purpose of Hajj or Umrah. The ruling applies to any pilgrim entering the city.
The context is pilgrimage, but the general recommendation applies to anyone entering Mecca.
2
Recognize the Ruling
The text states 'he begins with the Masjid al-Haram' – this is a mustahabb (recommended) action, not obligatory. The supplication is also mustahabb.
Mustahabb is synonymous with sunnah ghayr muakkadah in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Learn the Supplication
Memorize the supplication given in the text. It acknowledges Allah's lordship, the sanctity of the land, and asks for pardon and assistance in performing the rites.
The supplication is a model for humility and dependence on Allah.
4
Note the Etymology
Understand the linguistic derivation: 'Makkah' from 'makka' (to remove sins) and 'Bakkah' from 'yabtakka' (to crowd). This enriches the spiritual meaning.
The names reflect the city's sacred character and the crowd during tawaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإذا دخل مكة: ابتدأ بالمسجد الحرام). - سُميت مكة ؛ لأنها تَمُكُ الذنوب، أي تُذهِبُها. وتسمى أيضاً : بكة؛ لأن الناس يتباكون فيها، أي يزدحمون في الطواف. وقيل : بكة : اسم للمسجد، ومكة: اسم للبلد. والمستحب إذا دخل مكة أن يقول : اللهم أنت ربي وأنا عبدك، والبلد بلدك، جئتك هارباً منك إليك ؛ لأؤدي فرائضك، وأطلب رحمتك، وألتمس رضوانك، أسألك مسألة المضطرين إليك، الخائفين عقوبتك، أسألك أن تستقبلني اليوم بعفوك وتدخلني في رحمتك، وتتجاوز عني بمغفرتك، وتُعينني على أداء فرائضك.
His statement: (When he enters Makkah, he begins with the Masjid al-Haram). - It is called Makkah because it removes sins, i.e., eliminates them. It is also called Bakkah because people crowd in it, i.e., they press together in tawaf. And it is said: Bakkah is a name for the mosque, and Makkah is a name for the city. And it is recommended that when he enters Makkah, he says: 'O Allah, You are my Lord and I am Your servant, and the land is Your land; I have come to You fleeing from You to You, to perform Your obligations, seek Your mercy, and request Your pleasure. I ask You the question of those who are distressed to You, those who fear Your punishment. I ask You to receive me today with Your pardon, enter me into Your mercy, overlook me with Your forgiveness, and help me in performing Your obligations.'
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage describes the recommended actions upon entering Makkah. The pilgrim should first go to the Masjid al-Haram. The commentator then provides etymological notes: 'Makkah' is said to derive from 'makka' meaning to remove sins, and 'Bakkah' from 'yabtakka' meaning to crowd, referring to the tawaf. The recommended supplication upon entry is a profound plea to Allah, acknowledging His lordship, the sacredness of the land, and asking for pardon and assistance in performing Hajj duties.

Key Terms
مكة (Makkah): The holy city; named because it removes sins.
بكة (Bakkah): Another name for Mecca or specifically the Masjid al-Haram; derived from crowding for tawaf.
المسجد الحرام (al-Masjid al-Haram): The Sacred Mosque in Mecca, containing the Kaaba.
المستحب (al-mustahabb): Recommended; an action that is not obligatory but carries reward.

Recommended Times for Talbiyah During Hajj
Lists recommended occasions for reciting talbiyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four occasions when talbiyah is recommended according to the Hanafi school and explain the analogy to takbir in prayer that supports this recommendation.

Core Idea
Talbiyah is repeated at transitions: ascents, descents, meetings, and dawn — like takbir in prayer.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayira, a commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, lists specific occasions when the pilgrim should recite the talbiyah. Understanding these times helps you embody the spirit of ihram — constant remembrance at moments of change. The commentator explains that talbiyah functions like the takbir in prayer, marking movement from one state to another.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four occasions
From the text, list the four situations: ascending a high place, descending a valley, meeting riders, and at dawn (as-har). Memorize these as the core of the ruling.
The occasions relate to physical changes in the pilgrim's journey.
2
Understand the analogy to takbir
The text states that talbiyah in ihram is like takbir in prayer — it marks transitions. Just as takbir is said when moving between postures (standing, bowing, prostrating), talbiyah is recited when moving between states (e.g., climbing, descending).
This analogy (qiyas) strengthens the recommendation, making it a sunnah muakkadah.
3
Note the special emphasis on dawn
The commentator specifies 'as-har' (dawns) because it is a time when supplications are answered. This adds a spiritual dimension to the recommendation.
Dawn is a blessed time; reciting talbiyah then combines two acts of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلَّما علا شَرَفاً، أو هَبَط وادياً، أو لقي ركباناً، وبالأسحار .
قوله : ( وكلَّما علا شرَفاً) : أي صَعد مكاناً مرتفعاً.
قوله: (أو هَبَطَ وادياً، أو لقي ركباناً)؛ لأن التلبية في الإحرام: على مثال التكبير في الصلوات؛ للانتقال، فيؤتى بها عند الانتقال من حال إلى حال، وكذا عند الانتباه من النوم. كذا في «الينابيع».

And whenever he ascends a high place, or descends a valley, or meets riders, and at dawns. His statement: (and whenever he ascends a high place): meaning he climbs to an elevated place. His statement: (or descends a valley, or meets riders) because talbiyah in ihram is like the takbir in prayers for transitions; it is performed when moving from one state to another, and likewise when waking from sleep. Commentary
This passage is a ruling about the recommended times for reciting the talbiyah during Hajj. The author, based on the Mukhtasar al-Quduri, states that the pilgrim should recite talbiyah whenever he ascends a high place (sharaf), descends a valley (wadi), meets riders (rukban) on the road, and during the hours before dawn (as-har). The commentator clarifies that 'ascends a high place' means climbing to an elevated spot. The reason for reciting talbiyah at these moments is analogized to the takbir (Allahu Akbar) said during prayers at transitions: just as takbir marks movement from one posture to another, talbiyah marks the pilgrim's transition from one state to another in the journey. This also applies when waking from sleep. The specification of dawn is because it is a time when supplications are accepted. Thus, the talbiyah is not restricted to a single utterance but is recommended at multiple points of change during the pilgrimage.

Key Terms
التلبية (al-talbiyah): The formula 'Labbayk Allahumma Labbayk...' recited by a pilgrim in ihram.
شرف (sharaf): A high place or elevated ground.
أسحار (as-har): The latter part of the night before dawn, plural of sahr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Talbiyah is a recurring sunnah at times of transition: ascending, descending, meeting others, and at dawn, mirroring the takbir in prayer.

Entering Makkah and the Masjid al-Haram: Recommended Acts and Supplications
Ruling on entering Makkah and mosque with adab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended sequence when entering Makkah, recite the proper supplications, and identify the hukm (mustahabb/sunnah muakkadah) of these acts.

Core Idea
Upon entering Makkah, it is mustahabb to go first to the Haram after settling luggage and recite specific du'as.

This passage from the Quduri commentary details the proper etiquette for a pilgrim entering Makkah. It emphasizes that the first destination should be the Masjid al-Haram, after settling one's burdens. The text also explains the names Makkah and Bakkah and provides the recommended supplications for entering the city and the mosque. These are sunnah acts that enhance the spiritual experience of the pilgrimage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is entering Makkah and proceeding to the Masjid al-Haram. The ruling is mustahabb (sunnah muakkadah) – recommended, not obligatory.
2
Recognize the sequence
The pilgrim should first begin with the Masjid al-Haram after setting down his luggage so that his heart is free. Entering at night or day is equally permissible.
Setting down luggage ensures focus on worship.
3
Recite the du'a for entering Makkah
A full supplication is prescribed when entering Makkah, expressing humility, seeking forgiveness, and help in performing obligations.
The du'a is found in the source text.
4
Recite the du'a for entering the mosque
Upon entering the Masjid al-Haram, the pilgrim says another supplication acknowledging the sacredness of the House and seeking success in what pleases Allah.
5
Understand the names Makkah and Bakkah
Makkah means it erases sins; Bakkah (crowding) may refer to the mosque or the city. This adds spiritual significance to the location.

Seeing the Kaaba and Touching the Black Stone
Rulings and supplications when seeing the Kaaba and starting tawaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah acts when first seeing the Kaaba and when approaching the Black Stone, and apply the recommended supplications.

Core Idea
Seeing the Kaaba is a moment for heartfelt supplication; the pilgrim glorifies Allah and asks for mercy and peace.

This passage from Al-Jawharah Al-Nayirah outlines the sunnah adab for the pilgrim upon first sighting the Kaaba. It specifies the takbir and tahlil, a comprehensive du'a, and the manner of approaching the Black Stone. Understanding these recommended acts enriches the spiritual experience of tawaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial action upon seeing the Kaaba
The text states that when the pilgrim sees the House, they say 'Allahu Akbar' and 'La ilaha illa Allah'.
This establishes the first sunnah.
2
Extract the full recommended supplication
The commentary expands the utterance into a comprehensive du'a that includes praise, peace, faith, and following the Sunnah.
The du'a is a model for personal supplication.
3
Note the spiritual significance
The commentary explicitly mentions that dua at the sight of the Kaaba is answered (mustajab).
This encourages earnest supplication.
4
Describe the approach to the Black Stone
The pilgrim goes directly to the Black Stone, faces it, and repeats takbir and tahlil.
This marks the beginning of tawaf.
5
Recite the specific tahlil while walking
While moving from the door to the Stone, the pilgrim says a particular tahlil emphasizing tawhid and Allah's victory.
This enriches the transit.

Actions Upon Seeing the Kaaba and Approaching the Black Stone
Describes recommended acts and supplications at start of tawaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the recommended acts at the start of tawaf and explain the ruling of istilam (touching the Black Stone) according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Istilam of the Black Stone is a sunnah muakkadah, not an obligation, and must be performed without harming others.

When a pilgrim first sees the Kaaba and begins tawaf, there are specific recommended acts of worship, including supplications and the touching of the Black Stone. This lesson unpacks the rulings from the Hanafi text, clarifying what is sunnah and what is not, supported by a hadith on the Stone's testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequence of actions
The passage describes actions upon seeing the Kaaba (takbir, tahlil, du'a), entering the mosque, approaching the Black Stone, and performing istilam. Identify each stage and its prescribed acts.
2
Recognize the ruling level of each act
Most acts are sunnah muakkadah. Distinguish between sunnah (recommended) and wajib (obligatory). The text states that avoiding harm to Muslims is wajib, which overrides the sunnah of kissing if needed.
The hadith on the Stone's testimony is used to show the virtue of istilam, not its obligation.
3
Understand the method of istilam
Istilam involves facing the Stone, raising hands, placing palms on it, and kissing between the palms. If unable, one gestures and kisses their own palms.
This is analogous to takbirat al-ihram in prayer.
4
Analyze the condition of no harm
The text emphasizes that kissing is only done if it does not harm any Muslim. This is a condition that takes precedence.
The obligation of avoiding harm is stronger than the sunnah of kissing.
5
Grasp the proof and its implication
The hadith that the Stone will testify for those who touch it sincerely implies that the act is rewarded and thus recommended. This is the basis for considering it sunnah muakkadah.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) so it cannot establish an obligation.

قال في «النهاية»: استلام الحجر للطواف بمنزلة التكبير للصلاة، يبتدئ فيه الرجلُ طوافه ، قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «ليُبعثنَّ هذا الحَجَرُ يوم القيامة، وله عينان ينظر بهما ولسان ينطق به، يشهد لمن استلمه، واستقبله بالحق»(۱).

Chapter of Hajj
When he sees the House, he says takbir and tahlil.
Then he begins with the Black Stone, faces it, says takbir and tahlil: O Allah, save me from Your punishment and open for me the doors of Your mercy, and enter me into it, and protect me from the accursed Satan.

And his saying: He begins with the Sacred Mosque means after he has put down his baggage so that his heart (is free). And it does not harm him whether he enters Mecca at night or during the day.
When he enters the mosque, he says: O Allah, this House is Your House, the Sanctuary is Your Sanctuary, the slave is Your slave, and this is the station of the seeker of refuge with You from the Fire, so grant me success to what You love and are pleased with.

Chapter of Hajj
Page 341
And he raises his hands with the takbir, touches it, and kisses it if he can without harming any Muslim.
And there are other supplications besides these.
* His saying: (And he raises his hands with the takbir): The raising here is among the seven places, and he faces the inner side of his palms towards the Stone.
* His saying: (And touches it).
The manner of touching is that he places his palms on the Stone, puts his mouth between his palms, and kisses it if he can.
If he cannot, he directs his palms towards it and kisses his palms.
It is said in “an-Nihaya”: Touching the Stone for tawaf is like the takbir for prayer, the man begins his tawaf with it. The Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) said: “This Stone will surely be raised on the Day of Judgment, having two eyes with which it sees and a tongue with which it speaks, testifying for the one who touched it sincerely” (1).
His saying: (And kisses it if he can without harming any Muslim) because avoiding harm to a Muslim is obligatory.
(1) Sahih Ibn Khuzayma 4/221, Sahih Ibn Hibban (al-Ihsan) 9/25, Sunan Ibn Majah 2/982. An-Nawawi said in al-Majmu‘ 8/36: al-Bayhaqi narrated it with a sound chain according to the condition of the two Shaykhs, and Ibn Hajar in Fath al-Bari 3/462 strengthened it with its supporting evidence.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a sharh (commentary) on al-Quduri's matn, detailing the recommended actions when a pilgrim first sees the Kaaba and begins tawaf. It is divided into several rulings. First, upon catching sight of the Kaaba, the pilgrim should say takbir (Allahu Akbar) and tahlil (La ilaha illa Allah) and specific supplications. The author notes that supplication at this moment is answered. Then, the pilgrim moves toward the Black Stone, facing it, and again says takbir and tahlil, and recites additional dhikr while walking. At the Stone, he raises his hands with the takbir — one of seven places in Hajj where hands are raised, facing the inner palms toward the Stone — then performs istilam (touching) by placing his palms on the Stone, putting his mouth between them, and kissing it if possible. If unable to kiss it due to crowding, he may gesture toward it and then kiss his own hands. The author reinforces the importance of avoiding harm to fellow Muslims, as it is obligatory (wajib) to refrain from causing injury. A hadith is cited to show the significance of the Black Stone: it will be resurrected on the Day of Judgment with vision and speech, testifying for those who touched it with faith and sincerity. The commentary emphasizes that istilam at the start of tawaf is analogous to takbirat al-ihram in prayer, marking the beginning of the ritual.

Key Terms
استلام (istilām): Placing the palms on the Black Stone and kissing it, or gesturing towards it and kissing the hands if unable.
الحجر الأسود (al-ḥajar al-aswad): The Black Stone set in the eastern corner of the Kaaba, from which tawaf begins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi commentary, what is the ruling on touching and kissing the Black Stone?
A) Wajib (obligatory) because the Prophet did it.
B) Sunnah muakkadah (highly recommended) based on a hadith.
C) Haram (forbidden) because it involves shirk.
D) Mubah (permissible) with no special reward.

Key Takeaway
Touching and kissing the Black Stone is a highly recommended sunnah, but must be abandoned if it causes harm to any Muslim.

Rulings on Istilam and Kissing the Black Stone
Explanation of the manner and rulings of istilam and kissing the Black Stone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istilam and kissing the Black Stone, describe the proper manner, and explain the alternatives when direct action is not possible.

Core Idea
Istilam of the Black Stone is a sunnah; harming others to perform it is prohibited.

In the rituals of Hajj and Umrah, approaching the Black Stone (al-Hajar al-Aswad) to touch and kiss it is a highly recommended act. However, the Hanafi school clarifies that this is not obligatory; it is a sunnah (mustahabb). The Prophet's hadith about the Stone testifying for those who perform istilam with sincerity illustrates its virtue. At the same time, the clear condition is that one must not harm fellow Muslims in the process. This lesson examines the ruling, the evidence, and the practical alternatives when direct istilam is difficult.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage deals with two actions: raising hands with takbir (at the start of tawaf) and istilam (touching and kissing the Black Stone). Focus on istilam.
Ruling: sunnah (mustahabb), not wajib.
2
Locate the dalil
The author quotes a hadith from al-Nihaya: the Stone will be resurrected with senses and testify for those who touched it.
This is a proof for the virtue of istilam.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith uses the word 'yashhad' (testify), which indicates reward, not obligation. The conditional 'in istata'a' also shows it is not a mandatory act.
The hadith encourages but does not command.
4
Note the conditions
The condition is 'without harming a Muslim' because avoiding harm is obligatory.
This overrides the desire to kiss the Stone if it causes injury.
5
Identify the alternatives
If one cannot kiss the Stone directly, he may touch it with his palms and kiss his palms; or if even that is impossible (e.g., too crowded), he may simply gesture towards it.
The alternatives preserve the sunnah without causing harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحج
ورفع يديه مع التكبير، واستلمه، وقبله إن استطاع من غير أن يؤذي مسلماً.
وفيه أدعية غير هذه.
* قوله: (ورَفَعَ يديه مع التكبير ) : الرفع هنا: من السبع المواطن، ويستقبل بباطن كفيه إلى الحجر.
* قوله: (واستلمه).
صورة الاستلام أن يضع كفيه على الحجر، ويضع فمه بين كفيه، ويُقبله إن استطاع.
فإن لم يستطع : جعل كفيه نحوه، وقبل كفيه.
قال في «النهاية»: استلام الحجر للطواف بمنزلة التكبير للصلاة، يبتدئ فيه الرجلُ طوافه ، قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «ليُبعثنَّ هذا الحَجَرُ يوم القيامة، وله عينان ينظر بهما ولسان ينطق به، يشهد لمن استلمه، واستقبله بالحق»(۱).
قوله: (وقبله إن استطاع من غير أن يؤذي مسلماً)؛ لأن التحرز عن إيذاء المسلم واجب.
The Book of Hajj. And he raises his hands with the takbir, and touches it (the Black Stone), and kisses it if he is able, without harming a Muslim. And there are supplications other than these. * His statement: (and raises his hands with the takbir): raising here is from the seven places, and he faces the inner parts of his palms towards the Stone. * His statement: (and touches it). The form of touching (istilam) is that he places his palms on the Stone, and places his mouth between his palms, and kisses it if he is able. If he is unable, he directs his palms towards it and kisses his palms. He said in al-Nihaya: Istilam of the Stone for tawaf is like the takbir for prayer; a man begins his tawaf with it. The Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) said: 'This Stone will be resurrected on the Day of Resurrection, with two eyes with which it sees and a tongue with which it speaks, bearing witness for those who touched it and faced it with truth.' His statement: (and kisses it if he is able without harming a Muslim) because avoiding harm to a Muslim is obligatory.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof from the commentary on the Mukhtasar al-Quduri. It explains the sunnah actions related to the Black Stone at the start of tawaf. Ruling: Istilam (touching) and kissing the Black Stone are mustahabb (recommended), not wajib. The author emphasizes that one should avoid harming other Muslims when approaching the Stone. The hadith quoted is a proof for the virtue of istilam, stating that the Stone will testify for those who perform it sincerely. If one cannot reach the Stone, he may touch it with his hands and then kiss his hands, or if even that is difficult, he may gesture and say takbir. The statement from al-Nihaya compares istilam to takbir in prayer, highlighting its importance as a starting act.

Key Terms
الاستلام (al-istilam): Touching the Black Stone with one's palms, placing the mouth between them, and kissing it if possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pilgrim cannot kiss the Black Stone directly, what alternative is mentioned in the passage?
A) Kiss someone else's hand.
B) Touch with a stick and kiss it.
C) Direct palms towards the Stone and kiss the palms.
D) Simply gesture and not kiss anything.

Key Takeaway
Istilam and kissing the Black Stone are recommended acts that do not require harming others; alternatives exist when direct access is difficult.

Reversed Tawaf: Validity and Penalty in Hanafi Fiqh
Commentary on tawaf start and reversed tawaf dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reversed tawaf and explain why it differs from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Reversed tawaf is valid but requires a dam; the order is sunnah, not a pillar.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira discusses the proper start of tawaf and addresses a key inter-madhhab dispute: what happens if a pilgrim circumambulates in the opposite direction? The Hanafi school holds that the essential act is fulfilled, but the missed sunnah order must be compensated by a dam. The Shafi'i school, in contrast, considers the tawaf invalid. Let's analyze the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
First, recognize that the act is tawaf performed in reverse direction (starting from the Stone but going leftwards). The dispute is whether this tawaf is valid or invalid.
The text explicitly labels it 'al-tawaf al-mankus'.
2
State the Hanafi position
The commentary states: 'فإن أخذ عن يساره: أجزأه، وعليه دم' – it suffices (yujzi'uhu) but he must sacrifice a dam.
This shows the Hanafi distinction between essential validity and compensation.
3
State the Shafi'i opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the tawaf is not considered valid at all: 'وقال الشافعي: لا يُعتد بطوافه'.
The text quotes this directly.
4
Identify the underlying principle
The difference arises because Hanafis treat the order (tartib) as a sunnah, not a rukn. The essential act (tawaf itself) is fulfilled; compensation is for missing the sunnah. Shafi'is consider the order a pillar, so reversing invalidates it.
The commentary does not elaborate on the proofs, but this is the standard basis.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
A Hanafi must complete the reversed tawaf and later give a dam. A Shafi'i must repeat the tawaf from the beginning.
This affects what the mufti advises the pilgrim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أخذ عن يمينه مما يلي الباب، وقد اضطبع رداءه قبل ذلك، فيطوف بالبيت سبعة أشواط .
فإن لم يستطع تقبيله، ولا مسه بيده أمس الحجر شيئاً في يده من عرجُون أو غيره، ثم يقبل ذلك الشيء.
فإن لم يستطع شيئاً من ذلك : استقبله، وكبر وهلل.
- وهذا الاستقبال مستحب، وليس بواجب، يدل عليه قوله: إن استطاع، كما في قوله : ومس طيباً إن كان له.
* قوله : (ثم يأخذ عن يمينه مما يلي الباب): أي عن يمين الطائف، لا عن يمين الحجر.
فإن أخذ عن يساره: أجزأه ، وعليه دم، وهو الطواف المنكوس.
وقال الشافعي: لا يُعتد بطوافه.
* قوله: (وقد اضطبع رداءه قبل ذلك : أي اضطبع بردائه، وهو: أن يجعل رداءه تحت إبطه الأيمن، ويلقيه على كتفه الأيسر، ويُبدي منكبه الأيمن، ويغطي الأيسر.
- وهو سنة.
وسُمِّي اضطباعاً: لإبداء ضبعه، وهو عضده.
* قوله: (فيطوف بالبيت سبعة أشواط ، والشوط: من الحَجَر إلى الحجر.
Then he takes (the circumambulation) to his right side from what is next to the door, and he had already performed idtiba' of his wrap before that, so he makes tawaf around the House seven circuits. If he is unable to kiss it or touch it with his hand, he touches the Stone with something in his hand like a branch or other, then he kisses that thing. If he is unable to do any of that, he faces it and says takbir and tahlil. – This facing is recommended (mustahabb), not obligatory; this is indicated by his statement 'if he is able', as in his statement: 'and he touches perfume if he has it'. * His statement: (then he takes to his right side from what is next to the door): meaning to the right side of the circumambulator, not to the right of the Stone. If he takes to his left side, it suffices him, but he must sacrifice a dam, and this is called reversed tawaf (al-tawaf al-mankus). Al-Shafi'i said: His tawaf is not considered valid. * His statement: (and he had already performed idtiba' of his wrap before that): meaning he performs idtiba' with his wrap, which is: he places his wrap under his right armpit, throws it over his left shoulder, exposes his right shoulder, and covers his left shoulder. – This is a sunnah. It is called idtiba' because of exposing his ضبع (upper arm). * His statement: (then he makes tawaf around the House seven circuits), and a circuit (shawt) is from the Stone to the Stone.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is an inter_madhhab_dispute from the commentary on the Mukhtasar al-Quduri. It discusses the proper start of tawaf. Ruling: The pilgrim should begin tawaf from the Black Stone, taking a rightward direction (counterclockwise). He should have already performed idtiba' (uncovering the right shoulder) before starting. Idtiba' is a sunnah act specific to tawaf. The dispute concerns reversed tawaf (tawaf mankus) where one goes leftwards. The Hanafi position: it is valid (yujzi'uhu) but requires a dam (sacrifice). The Shafi'i position: it is invalid and must be repeated. The text also clarifies that the 'right side' refers to the circumambulator's right, not the Stone's right. It affirms that gestures like saying takbir are mustahabb, not wajib. Finally, it defines a shawt (circuit) as from the Stone back to the Stone.

Key Terms
الاضطباع (al-idtiba'): Placing the lower garment under the right armpit, over the left shoulder, exposing the right shoulder and covering the left.
الطواف المنكوس (al-tawaf al-mankus): Circumambulation performed by going leftwards (opposite direction), starting from the Stone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a pilgrim performs tawaf by going leftwards (reversed tawaf)?
A) The tawaf is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The tawaf is valid but he must sacrifice a dam.
C) The tawaf is valid without any penalty because the direction is not required.
D) The tawaf is invalid and he must give a dam.

Key Takeaway
Reversed tawaf is valid in the Hanafi school but requires a dam; the Shafi'i school considers it invalid due to differing views on the nature of the direction requirement.

Starting Tawaf from the Right and the Ruling of Tawaf Mankus
Detailed rulings on starting tawaf, idtiba', and the dispute over tawaf in reverse order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on starting tawaf from the right, identify the sunnah of idtiba', and apply the distinction between fard and wajib to evaluate the validity of tawaf mankus.

Core Idea
Starting tawaf from the right is wajib; omitting it does not invalidate but requires a dam.

In this passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, the commentator explains the initial acts of tawaf, focusing on the direction of start and the sunnah of idtiba'. A key point of contention with the Shafi'i school arises regarding tawaf performed in reverse order (tawaf mankus). By understanding the Hanafi distinction between fard (pillar) and wajib (obligatory element), we see why a reversed tawaf remains valid yet requires compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on starting direction
Read the text: 'ثم أخذ عن يمينه مما يلي الباب'. The commentator clarifies that 'right' means the right of the circumambulator. Determine the legal status of starting from the right.
The text uses an imperative verb, but the commentator later indicates that omission only requires a dam.
2
Understand idtiba' as sunnah
Locate the description of idtiba' and note the commentator explicitly says it is sunnah. Distinguish this from the wajib nature of starting direction.
Idtiba' is a sunnah, not wajib, so omitting it is permissible without compensation.
3
Examine tawaf mankus
Find the sentence: 'فإن أخذ عن يساره: أجزأه، وعليه دم'. This is the key ruling on tawaf mankus. Note the phrase 'أجزأه' (it suffices him) and the required dam.
The text uses 'أجزأه' to indicate validity, which contrasts with invalidation.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The text itself provides the proof: it says 'أجزأه' (suffices) and 'عليه دم' (upon him a dam). The Hanafi school uses this to conclude that the act is valid but compensation is due. The commentator's elaboration on the phrase يدل عليه قوله reinforces this.
The proof is that the text explicitly separates validity from the need for compensation, establishing the act as wajib, not rukn.
5
Compare with Shafi'i position
Note the objection: 'وقال الشافعي: لا يعتد بطوافه'. Understand that the Shafi'i school considers the order (tartib) a rukn, so reversal invalidates. Contrast with Hanafi view.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is that a Hanafi does not repeat tawaf but must give dam; a Shafi'i repeats.

وقال الشافعي): لا يُعتد بطوافه.

الأيمن، ويغطي الأيسر.

Then he takes to his right from the side of the door, and he has placed his cloak under his right armpit before that, and he circumambulates the House seven circuits. If he cannot kiss it (the Black Stone) nor touch it with his hand, he touches the Stone with something in his hand from a date-stalk or other, then he kisses that object. If he cannot do any of that, he faces it and says 'Allahu Akbar' and 'La ilaha illallah'. And this facing is recommended, not obligatory, as indicated by his saying: 'if he can', just as in his saying: 'and he touches perfume if he has it.' * His saying: (Then he takes to his right from the side of the door): meaning to the right of the circumambulator, not to the right of the Stone. If he takes to his left, it suffices him, and he owes a sacrificial animal, and it is the 'reversed circumambulation' (tawaf mankus). Al-Shafi'i said: His circumambulation is not considered. * His saying: (And he has placed his cloak under his right armpit before that): meaning he places his cloak under his right armpit, and throws it over his left shoulder, baring his right shoulder and covering the left. This is Sunnah. It is called 'idtiba'' because of baring his 'dhabi' (upper arm). * His saying: (He circumambulates the House seven circuits), and a circuit (shaut) is from the Stone to the Stone.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira expounds on the initial acts of tawaf. It begins with the ruling that the pilgrim should start tawaf from the right side, i.e., from the direction of the door of the Ka'ba, as indicated by the author. The commentator clarifies that 'right' refers to the right of the circumambulator, not the right of the Black Stone. If one mistakenly starts from the left, the tawaf is still valid (jaza) but a dam (sacrificial animal) is due; this is called 'tawaf mankus' (reversed tawaf). Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, holding that such a tawaf is not valid at all. The Hanafi position is that the obligation is satisfied, but the direction is a wajib (required) act whose omission necessitates a dam. Next, the passage details the sunnah of idtiba': placing the upper garment under the right armpit and over the left shoulder, exposing the right shoulder. This is sunnah, not fard. The commentator then defines the circuit (shaut) as from the Stone to the Stone. The passage also covers the recommended acts for greeting the Black Stone: kissing, touching, or pointing and takbir.

Key Terms
اضطباع (idtiba'): Placing the upper garment under the right armpit and over the left shoulder, baring the right shoulder.
طواف منكوس (tawaf mankus): Circumambulation performed starting from the left side instead of the right, considered reversed.
شوط (shaut): One complete circuit from the Black Stone back to the Black Stone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The tawaf is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The tawaf is valid but a dam (sacrificial animal) is due.
C) The tawaf is valid and no compensation is required.
D) The tawaf is invalid but no repetition is needed.

Key Takeaway
Starting tawaf from the right is wajib; if omitted, the tawaf remains valid but a dam is due.
